    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'

        if (this.up('#box')) {

            swapContent(lessLink);

<span id="freeTextContainer17147877772556452058">Nine Stories (1953) is a collection of short stories by American fiction writer J. D. Salinger published in April 1953. It includes two of his most famous short stories, "A Perfect Day for Bananafish" and "For Esmé – with Love and Squalor". (Nine Stories is the U.S. title; the book is published in many other countries as For Esmé - with Love and Squalor, and Other Stories.</span>
  <span id="freeText17147877772556452058" style="display:none">Nine Stories (1953) is a collection of short stories by American fiction writer J. D. Salinger published in April 1953. It includes two of his most famous short stories, "A Perfect Day for Bananafish" and "For Esmé – with Love and Squalor". <div id="review_30308482" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/30308482" />
    <a title="brian   " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/193310-brian"><img alt="brian   " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1353195524p2/193310.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/30308482?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="brian   " class="user" itemprop="url" name="brian   " href="/user/show/193310-brian">brian   </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to brian by: </span>
      david 
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer30308482" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5776717179457391619">i know of three people who are totally obsessed with j.d. salinger:<br/> <br/>john hinckley<br/>mark david chapman<br/>goodreads david <br/><br/>i know of four reasons why i (must) love this book:<br/><br/>1) because i don't want to see a list that looks like this:<br/><br/>ronald reagan<br/>john lennon<br/>goodreads brian<br/><br/><br/>2) because in the early 80s salinger was a huge fan of the sitcom <i>mr. merlin</i> which was based on the premise -- wait for it… wait for it... -- that merlin (yeah, that merlin) is alive and well in san francisco and working as a mechanic. </span>
  <span id="freeText5776717179457391619" style="display:none">i know of three people who are totally obsessed with j.d. salinger:<br /> <br />john hinckley<br />mark david chapman<br />goodreads david <br /><br />i know of four reasons why i (must) love this book:<br /><br />1) because i don't want to see a list that looks like this:<br /><br />ronald reagan<br />john lennon<br />goodreads brian<br /><br /><br />2) because in the early 80s salinger was a huge fan of the sitcom <i>mr. merlin</i> which was based on the premise -- wait for it… wait for it... -- that merlin (yeah, that merlin) is alive and well in san francisco and working as a mechanic. <br /><br />and it gets better: salinger became totally obsessed with elaine joyce, the lead actress from the show, and came out of hiding to track her down and date her. <br /><br />joyce could later be seen on just about every single game show and… well, just watch this clip:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=HywZDxNWUBc">http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=HywZDx...</a><br /><br />(yeah, you really gotta love charles nelson reilly)<br /><br />i imagine salinger, lonely, smelly, the bottom of his too large t-shirt hard with encrusted sperm, top of it soft with drool… beard stubble, cat hair, spoiled milk, stale danish, waiting all week for the chance to tug at his old man penis to 23 minutes of mr. merlin, hoarsely shouting in anger and frustration as he’s about to ejaculate and they abruptly cut away from joyce to merlin. (thank god for tivo and being able to freeze frame or slo-mo marisa tomei without having to hoarsely shout at ethan hawke and phillip seymour hoffman)<br /><br />so, it’s very funny, of course, but also incredibly human and poignant and tragic. and while the tendency is to ridicule salinger for falling for a third-rate sitcom actress, it can’t help but humanize and endear him to any of us who have totally, completely, and inexplicably fallen for someone…<br /><br /><br />3) because i'm a shameless contrarian and all you fuckers love to rag on the man. so i really wanted to love this book. and it wasn't difficult. <br /><br /><br />4) because it's great. these stories are great. and they don’t even feel like stories, but like nine strange impressionist sketches. i almost feel that each story should have started and ended with an ellipse... you kind of flow from one weird, fragmented sketch to the next -- from <i>the laughing man</i>, which makes you feel more like a child than any story you’ve ever read, into <i>bananafish</i> which is loaded with more stunning and surreal imagery than should be allowed in one story, and then to <i>Teddy</i>’s strange world of cruise ships and fate and genius children…<br /><br />get in the ring, motherfuckers!<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5776717179457391619" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10445822544334498136">قبلا ناتور دشت رو خونده بودم و حالم از شخصیت هولدن بهم خورده بود و با خودم عهد کرده بودم دیگه دنبال کتاب هایی از این نویسنده نرم که اصولا جو گیرها توی وبلاگ هاشون با آب و تاب میگن عالیه ! هرچند دوباره تحت تاثیر جو این کتاب رو شروع کردم و اوایلش به خودم میگفتم که باز وقتت با یه کتاب نامناسب حروم شد ؛ اما با خوندن چندتا داستان دیگه تازه دستم اومد سلینجر سبکش چطوریه و یک حالت سوییچ بین کودکی و بزرگسالی سر تا سر داستان هاش به چشم میخورد حالا یا به صورت محسوس یا نا محسوس . داستان های "تقدیم به ازمه ب</span>
  <span id="freeText10445822544334498136" style="display:none">قبلا ناتور دشت رو خونده بودم و حالم از شخصیت هولدن بهم خورده بود و با خودم عهد کرده بودم دیگه دنبال کتاب هایی از این نویسنده نرم که اصولا جو گیرها توی وبلاگ هاشون با آب و تاب میگن عالیه ! هرچند دوباره تحت تاثیر جو این کتاب رو شروع کردم و اوایلش به خودم میگفتم که باز وقتت با یه کتاب نامناسب حروم شد ؛ اما با خوندن چندتا داستان دیگه تازه دستم اومد سلینجر سبکش چطوریه و یک حالت سوییچ بین کودکی و بزرگسالی سر تا سر داستان هاش به چشم میخورد حالا یا به صورت محسوس یا نا محسوس . داستان های "تقدیم به ازمه با عشق و نکبت" و "دلتنگی های نقاش خیابان چهل و هشتم" و "تدی" ! خصوصا داستان "تدی" مورد علاقه ی من بود ... خود داستان تدی به اندازه کافی ارزش تک خونده شدن رو داره و مفاهیمش و گنجوندن بزرگی یک روح در یک کودک ده ساله توسط سلینجر برام خیلی جالب بود و نظرم در مورد این نویسنده حالا کمی بهتره ، امیدوارم اگر باز کتابی ازش به دستم رسید این نظر تقریبا مثبتم پس رفت نکنه بلکه از طرفدارهاش هم بشم ، البته امیدوارم ...</span>

<div id="review_2690840" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2690840" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/166376-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1345482402p2/166376.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2690840?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/166376-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2690840" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12123271709946406472">If I can get serious for a moment, and cast aside the brittle, smartassed, persona that the social networking aspect of goodreads tends to bring out, I'd like to try to express what it is that drives me in this life. It is the following belief, instilled primarily by my mother, an exceptionally smart woman who never suffered fools gladly, but had the mitigating grace to be one of the warmest, most generous women you could ever hope to meet, as well as having one of the greatest voices you can im</span>
  <span id="freeText12123271709946406472" style="display:none">If I can get serious for a moment, and cast aside the brittle, smartassed, persona that the social networking aspect of goodreads tends to bring out, I'd like to try to express what it is that drives me in this life. It is the following belief, instilled primarily by my mother, an exceptionally smart woman who never suffered fools gladly, but had the mitigating grace to be one of the warmest, most generous women you could ever hope to meet, as well as having one of the greatest voices you can imagine <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/story/show/2949?chapter=1" rel="nofollow noopener"> (Buttercup) </a> <br /><br />Here's the main thing she taught me: each of us has an inescapable responsibility to take whatever talent we have been given on this earth, and to develop it as far and as well as life allows.<br /><br />This is so deeply ingrained in my beliefs that I can pretty much trace every major decision I've made in my life back to it.<br /><br />What does this have to do with the price of eggs? Well, it's the reason Jerome David Salinger makes me as mad as all get out. Because I can certainly understand why, given the perfection of the stories in this collection, any writer might not want to risk spoiling his reputation by following up with work that might not reach the same level. Hell, nothing could possibly reach the perfection of the stories, "For Esme - with Love and Squalor", "The Laughing Man", "Down by the Dinghy", or "Just Before the War with the Eskimos". And while I'm not really a great fan of Seymour Glass, "A Perfect Day for Bananafish" is pretty damned awesome as well.<br /><br />So, yeah, J.D. - after those stories, it's hard to imagine anything better. Even anything comparable.<br /><br />But that's still no excuse for not trying, you arrogant egotistical bastard. You were dealt a monumental, unimaginable, talent. And for you to squat there in-fucking-communicado in your bloody bunker in New England, resting on your admittedly golden freaking laurels, is an act of unconscionable, unpardonable, selfishness. I could almost convince myself that your genius crossing over into madness was the explanation for your lack of output, but you seem craftily able to sic your lawyers on anyone perceived to encroach on your goddamned "privacy".<br /><br />So, while I can understand the impulse of not wanting to risk your reputation, I sure as hell can't forgive it. You were granted an incredible gift. You should be using it.<br /><br />And, sorry folks, it's far beyond me to locate exactly where the genius lies in the particular stories mentioned. You really just need to read them for yourselves.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12123271709946406472" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1753980022" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10015217599398540759">This is as good of a short story collection that one could hope to find. Salinger was a heck of a writer, certainly well known for his classic, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5107.The_Catcher_in_the_Rye' title='The Catcher in the Rye by J.D. Salinger' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Catcher in the Rye</a>, but there is much more out there, like this little jewel for example. I give this 5 stars on the strength of two stories alone, but they all were good. The two stories I mention are <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7875439.A_Perfect_Day_for_Bananafish' title='A Perfect Day for Bananafish by J.D. Salinger' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Perfect Day for Bananafish</a>, and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22836075.For_Esme___With_Love_And_Squalor' title='For Esme - With Love And Squalor by J.D. Salinger' rel='nofollow noopener'>For Esme - With Love And Squalor</a>. Both have themes involving troubled soldiers returning from World War II. Salinger</span>
  <span id="freeText10015217599398540759" style="display:none">This is as good of a short story collection that one could hope to find. Salinger was a heck of a writer, certainly well known for his classic, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5107.The_Catcher_in_the_Rye" title="The Catcher in the Rye by J.D. Salinger" rel="nofollow noopener">The Catcher in the Rye</a>, but there is much more out there, like this little jewel for example. I give this 5 stars on the strength of two stories alone, but they all were good. The two stories I mention are <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7875439.A_Perfect_Day_for_Bananafish" title="A Perfect Day for Bananafish by J.D. Salinger" rel="nofollow noopener">A Perfect Day for Bananafish</a>, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22836075.For_Esme___With_Love_And_Squalor" title="For Esme - With Love And Squalor by J.D. Salinger" rel="nofollow noopener">For Esme - With Love And Squalor</a>. Both have themes involving troubled soldiers returning from World War II. Salinger's experiences in the war certainly influenced his writing, and may have been partly responsible for his reclusiveness for the last 45 years of his life.<br /><br />Update: September 2017 is the release of the movie "Rebel in The Rye", which is based on the autobiography <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8822656.J_D__Salinger_A_Life" title="J.D. Salinger A Life by Kenneth Slawenski" rel="nofollow noopener">J.D. Salinger: A Life</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2902032.Kenneth_Slawenski" title="Kenneth Slawenski" rel="nofollow noopener">Kenneth Slawenski</a>. I look forward to reading the book and seeing the movie to learn more about this interesting man.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_59195068" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/59195068" />
    <a title="Paul Bryant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant"><img alt="Paul Bryant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1224113172p2/416390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/59195068?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Bryant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Bryant" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant">Paul Bryant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/416390-paul-bryant?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer59195068" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4837553631730855375">If kidnappers had snatched up J D Salinger some time in the early 1970s, driven like madmen through the night and the next day too and imprisoned him in a small but pleasant room somewhere near Boise, furnished him with with all mod cons, and told him he wasn't going anyplace soon until he'd finished at the very least another nine stories, and at best three or four complete novels; and if the kidnappers - due to an endearing cocktail of naivete and compassion (because you know they were just lit</span>
  <span id="freeText4837553631730855375" style="display:none">If kidnappers had snatched up J D Salinger some time in the early 1970s, driven like madmen through the night and the next day too and imprisoned him in a small but pleasant room somewhere near Boise, furnished him with with all mod cons, and told him he wasn't going anyplace soon until he'd finished at the very least another nine stories, and at best three or four complete novels; and if the kidnappers - due to an endearing cocktail of naivete and compassion (because you know they were just literature fans like you and me, not blank-eyed killers, and they weren't entirely convinced about this whole caper to begin with let it be said) let JD go for long walks (to get inspiration, but really to beat on a nearby farmhouse door and call the cops); and if they were then rounded up (not too hard, said the cops) and put on trial - not a jury in the land would have convicted them.<br /> When the prosecution rested and the defence opened, their lawyer would simply have issued a copy of Nine Stories to all 12 jurors and said "Ladies and gentlemen, I rest my case."<br /><br />This is not to say that each of the Nine is such a great golden glowing nugget of controlled power, insight and wisdom (some are) but that the whole is such eloquent proof of the perspicacity, intelligence and all-round humanbeingness of JDS that reading this collection is very bittersweet - how lovely it all is, and how very little of it there is, when duller, pudgier-fingered writers type on, and on, and publish, and publish.<br /> Anyone who has encountered comments by myself on Ye Olde Catcher in Ye Rye will now accuse me of inconsistency, or at least, be expecting me to accuse JDS of the same. How can I hate the novel for its unbearable whine and Johnny-one-note somebody-shut-him-up-please tiresomeness and yet enjoy all the rest of JDS as I do? They're cut from the same cloth, it's not like Picasso's blue period and Picasso the cubist which could have been different guys, or the Velvet Underground's first and third albums which could have been a different band. But I've come across this in different areas of the universe - can't stand Tom Waits until Swordfishtrombones, think he's a genius for three albums, then can't stand him again. Shakespeare's tragedies - oui! Shakespeare's comedies - er, non! So maybe not that unusual.<br /><br />JDS famously published all his stuff between 1951 and 1963 and then STOPPED. (Which is why the kidnappers pounced, they gave him a good ten year rest and that was ENOUGH to their way of thinking.) And he stopped just as things were getting really interesting. He writes of the murderous conformities of American educated middle-class life and of the outcasts and especially young kids who either subvert this button-down world or bail out swiftly. Just as he stopped publishing things began to change. the 60s began swinging, and the youthquake (as it has been termed) was upon us. Just the very stuff that you might have thought would have fascinated JD. What do the kids do when they try to make their own rules up? I feel the absence of JDS throughout the 60s and 70s, as i feel the absence of another American writer who STOPPED in 1963, Sylvia Plath. I want to know what these two clever clogs would have made of the tumultuous ten years which followed the self-stilling of their voices. <br /><br />But back to the Nine Stories - and to steal a fellow reviewer's catch-phrase:<br /><br />Is it a classic? <br /><br />Answer : Yes. Goddamn!<br /><br /><br />PS : I realise I also speculated upon the advisability of kidnapping Thomas Bernhard elsewhere but that was to save the world from any further novels like Extinction, whereas the JD Salinger kidnap is for the opposite reason. But I would like to publicly state that I do not condone the imprisonment of any writers for any reasons, so please don't try this at home.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4837553631730855375" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1106598614" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1625077303000087043"><i>
  <b>
    “Each of his phrases was rather like a little ancient island, inundated by a miniature sea of whiskey.”
  </b>
</i><br/><br/><b>Oh Mr. Salinger, why couldn’t you have published more of these amazing stories in your life time???</b><br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1457080123i/18315678._SY540_.jpg' alt='Esme' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><i>
  <b>Nine Stories</b>
</i> -- a collection of brilliant short stories from J.D. Salinger. It is in this collection where the Glass family, the main constituents of <i>
  <b>Franny and Zooey,</b>
</i> is first introduced. In the next eight stories, we meet and get to know characters with an assortment of menta</span>
  <span id="freeText1625077303000087043" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>
    “Each of his phrases was rather like a little ancient island, inundated by a miniature sea of whiskey.”
  </b>
</i><br><br><b>Oh Mr. Salinger, why couldn’t you have published more of these amazing stories in your life time???</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1457080123i/18315678._SY540_.jpg" alt="Esme" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>
  <b>Nine Stories</b>
</i> -- a collection of brilliant short stories from J.D. Salinger. It is in this collection where the Glass family, the main constituents of <i>
  <b>Franny and Zooey,</b>
</i> is first introduced. In the next eight stories, we meet and get to know characters with an assortment of mental and physical ailments, and self-discoveries.<br><br>This is my second journey with Salinger after <i>
  <b>Franny and Zooey.</b>
</i> My favorites here are <i>
  <b>To Esme – With Love and Squalor,</b>
</i> <i>
  <b>The Laughing Man,</b>
</i> <i>
  <b>De Daumier-Smith’s Blue Period,</b>
</i> and <i>
  <b>Teddy.</b>
</i> A shared thread through all nine stories is the mood of desperation, of frustration, and of higgledy-piggledy identities. The characters are very real; these are real people with real issues starting to overspill into their everyday lives.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1457083343i/18316147._SY540_.jpg" alt="Mask" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>These stories haunt me. I found <i>
  <b>To Esme – With Love and Squalor</b>
</i> a story about the effects of war on an individual stayed with me for days. It’s so simply written, and yet, packs so much emotion and observation on the state of war and the mental and physical drain it can take on one person. From the one line note about a twitch on the face, to a shaky hand, the subtle differences from the first half of the story to the second half create an overall dreadful vision.<br><br>What is <i>
  <b>De Daumier-Smith’s Blue Period</b>
</i> -- loneliness, isolation, misrepresentation, reinvention, escape, connection? Who is Jean De Daumier-Smith -- we never really know since this the name the narrator calls himself. The fact that we never know Jean’s real name is significant; it serves to highlight the idea of misrepresentation and reinvention. Jean appears to be uncomfortable with who he is and by changing his name Salinger allows Jean to reinvent himself. The trigger for Jean wishing to reinvent himself stems from the loneliness and isolation that he feels possibly due to his mother’s death. By reinventing himself, Jean is able to escape from the painful realities of the world around him. We, all of us, can relate.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1457083343i/18316148.jpg" alt="Blue" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>This collection of stories should be read over and over again. When I next read these stories I’ll discover something new about one of the characters or catch a new allusion or reference. What insights will I glean about the Glass family?<br><br>I could go on forever about the themes here. I could write pages about these people. I wonder where Esme is now. What will become of Teddy? Does the Chief find love and is he actually The Laughing Man? <br><br>It's what's left unsaid here that really intrigues. Words may go unuttered, but still one hopes ... <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1457083343i/18316149.jpg" alt="Teddy" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="1625077303000087043" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_175415" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/175415" />
    <a title="Rolls" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18657-rolls"><img alt="Rolls" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1478844909p2/18657.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/175415?book_show_action=true">Mar 06, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rolls" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rolls" href="/user/show/18657-rolls">Rolls</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of rainy days, Belle and Sebastian and Wes Anderson</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer175415" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1417612168364106519">Salinger's "Nine Stories" should be renamed "How to Write Short Stories." While many hold up "Catcher in the Rye" as the zenith of his achievements for me it will always be this wistful and brave little book. I re-read it two or three times or year. I love it that much. <br/><br/>To be honest out of the nine stories collected here I would say that only a third are Salinger's best. "Perfect Day for Banafish," "For Esme - With Love and Squalor," and "The Laughing Man" are to me the peaks of short fiction. E</span>
  <span id="freeText1417612168364106519" style="display:none">Salinger's "Nine Stories" should be renamed "How to Write Short Stories." While many hold up "Catcher in the Rye" as the zenith of his achievements for me it will always be this wistful and brave little book. I re-read it two or three times or year. I love it that much. <br /><br />To be honest out of the nine stories collected here I would say that only a third are Salinger's best. "Perfect Day for Banafish," "For Esme - With Love and Squalor," and "The Laughing Man" are to me the peaks of short fiction. Everything that Salinger does best he does in these three tales. Nobody wrote children better than him. They leap off the page at you right into your lap. Esme, her brother, Seymour's little friend and the narrator of "Laughing Man" are so vivid and real you feel like running them all down the street for ice cream and cake. They are that true to life. <br /><br />Same goes for Seymour in "Banana Fish" and the narrator of "For Esme." Nobody got into the heads of brilliant but troubled young people better than Salinger. What we hear about Seymour as opposed to what we see creates a palpable (and beautiful)tension. The narrator of "For Esme"'s war inflicted emotional problems are drawn with such artistry as to flood over you as you read. <br /><br />"Uncle Wiggly in Connecticut," "Daumier-Smith's Blue Period," and "War with the Eskimos" to me fall into the "damn entertaining but not great" category. These stories are beautifully observed, funny, poignant and always a pleasure to read but lack that magic the first three have to spare. Of course that being said even being good but not great Salinger makes them better than most.<br /><br />Finally "Teddy," "Down in the Dinghy" and "Pretty Mouth Green My Eyes" are good stories but I feel they suffer from being collected in the same book as the others. Each alone is enthralling but not a one of them is a patch on "Esme," or "Bananafish." Where the other stories feel like a full meal these come off more like snacks. Tasty but not quite filling.<br /><br />If you like Salinger and want to read something by him that won't make you want to shoot a president or a sixties rock star this my friend is the book for you.<br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="1417612168364106519" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2058758335" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2058758335" />
    <a title="Robin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36555254-robin"><img alt="Robin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536187488p2/36555254.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2058758335?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robin" href="/user/show/36555254-robin">Robin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=american">american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2058758335" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1873112672163738503">Adverbs. It's all because of adverbs that I read this collection. I asked a wonderful teacher of mine about adverbs (whether to use them, and all that), and the main gist of his answer was: "Read <i>Nine Stories</i> by J.D. Salinger. He's the master of the adverb."<br/><br/>Good lord, he is. The almost 200 page collection is positively (see what I did there?) LITTERED with them. One beautifully (ha!) placed adverb after the next. In one paragraph I counted five. Five gorgeous adverbs in a single, solitary paragr</span>
  <span id="freeText1873112672163738503" style="display:none">Adverbs. It's all because of adverbs that I read this collection. I asked a wonderful teacher of mine about adverbs (whether to use them, and all that), and the main gist of his answer was: "Read <i>Nine Stories</i> by J.D. Salinger. He's the master of the adverb."<br /><br />Good lord, he is. The almost 200 page collection is positively (see what I did there?) LITTERED with them. One beautifully (ha!) placed adverb after the next. In one paragraph I counted five. Five gorgeous adverbs in a single, solitary paragraph. And it works - oh, how it works - <i>magnificently</i>. (Take that, Stephen King.)<br /><br />Besides the adverbs, I found two of the most incredible short stories I've ever read: <i>A Perfect Day for Bananafish</i> and <i>For Esmé - with Love and Squalor</i>. Both of these stories left me with a catch in my throat, my pulse racing (and not just because of the adverbs), and a compulsion to peel the pages back and re-experience the power and emotions through this man's cunningly chosen words. I tried to explain <i>Esmé</i> to my mother and found myself choking up with tears.<br /><br />Interestingly (I could do this all day), both stories are similar, though one is devastating and the other hopeful. Both involve a post WWII soldier suffering from PTSD. Both involve the absolute delightful innocence of a child. Both feature the most perfect dialogue. Actually, all of the nine stories feature dialogue. I'm going to have to re-read this one day, just to study the dialogue. One of the stories is almost 100% one side of a telephone call. I mean, this guy was brilliant. I just wish he'd written more.<br /><br />Not all the stories contain the potency of the two I mentioned. But each story deserves to be read thoughtfully and enjoyed fully, methodically, even reverently. <br /><br />5 stars, for Esmé.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1873112672163738503" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2058758335">

<span id="freeTextContainer403863432612676576"><br/>نمدونم ترجمه یه نارسایی‌هایی داشت واقعاً یا نه، ولی ویرایشش یه‌‌کم اعصابمو خرد می‌کرد. کتابی که اینقد تجدید چاپ شده اونم. سلینجر اونم. داستان‌کوتاهاش اونم. سلینجر برا من یه جورایی مث دوا شده. هرازگاهی باید یه چیزی ازش بخونم که دوباره ریکاوری بشم. چندتا از داستان‌های این مجموعه رو قبلاً هم خونده بودم(مثل یک روز خوش برای موز ماهی و دهانم فلان و چشمانم چنان :همر: خدایی چه مناسبتی داشت این اسم من نفهمیدم!) و کلاً دوست داشتم. بازم اینو خواهم خوند. ولی نه اینطور پشت سر هم. هر داستانو دیگه می‌کشه می‌رم</span>
  <span id="freeText403863432612676576" style="display:none"><br />نمدونم ترجمه یه نارسایی‌هایی داشت واقعاً یا نه، ولی ویرایشش یه‌‌کم اعصابمو خرد می‌کرد. کتابی که اینقد تجدید چاپ شده اونم. سلینجر اونم. داستان‌کوتاهاش اونم. سلینجر برا من یه جورایی مث دوا شده. هرازگاهی باید یه چیزی ازش بخونم که دوباره ریکاوری بشم. چندتا از داستان‌های این مجموعه رو قبلاً هم خونده بودم(مثل یک روز خوش برای موز ماهی و دهانم فلان و چشمانم چنان :همر: خدایی چه مناسبتی داشت این اسم من نفهمیدم!) و کلاً دوست داشتم. بازم اینو خواهم خوند. ولی نه اینطور پشت سر هم. هر داستانو دیگه می‌کشه می‌رم می‌خونم. مثلاً تدی رو می‌دونم دیری نمی‌گذره که می‌رم باز می‌خونم. عجب فضایی داشت. </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789753630484" href="/book/show/10362243-dokuz-yk">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/67914616-mevsim-yenice?shelf=2018-exact">2018-exact</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2162524874" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5831205714961664346">Bir süredir kitap seçimlerimden yana oldukça şanslı olduğumu hissediyorum. Ama yine de itiraf etmek gerekirse, uzun bir süredir beni bu kadar etkileyen, çiviyle yerime çakan öyküler okumamıştım. <br/><br/>Yıllardır rafta okunmayı bekleyen kitaplardan biriydi Salinger’ın Dokuz Öyküsü. İyi ki beklemiş. Benim gibi öykü delisi biri için, bazı öykülerin, bazı atmosferlerin doğru zamanı var gibi geliyor hep. Mesela 10 yıl önce okumuş olsam araya gidebilecek bir kitaptı benim için bu, o nedenle tam da şu günlerd</span>
  <span id="freeText5831205714961664346" style="display:none">Bir süredir kitap seçimlerimden yana oldukça şanslı olduğumu hissediyorum. Ama yine de itiraf etmek gerekirse, uzun bir süredir beni bu kadar etkileyen, çiviyle yerime çakan öyküler okumamıştım. <br /><br />Yıllardır rafta okunmayı bekleyen kitaplardan biriydi Salinger’ın Dokuz Öyküsü. İyi ki beklemiş. Benim gibi öykü delisi biri için, bazı öykülerin, bazı atmosferlerin doğru zamanı var gibi geliyor hep. Mesela 10 yıl önce okumuş olsam araya gidebilecek bir kitaptı benim için bu, o nedenle tam da şu günlerde okumuş olmaktan çok memnunum. <br /><br />Ayrıntılarda boğulabileceği, kolaylıkla ajite edebileceği her konuyu ve sahneyi öyle güzel göğüslemiş ki hayran olmamak elde değil. Öykülerin içine yerleştirdiği önemsiz gözüken ufacık bir anın, göz kırpan bir ayrıntının, dönüp dolaşıp muhteşem sona hizmet edecek sağlam birer yapı taşına dönüşmesi, okuyucu olarak tam bir doyum sağladı bende. Her öykünün bitiminde aradığımı hem verdi hem de peşinden koşturttu Salinger. Yaşadığımı hissettirdi kısaca. <br /><br />Dokuz öykünün hemen hepsinde, çocuk ve yetişkin perspektifi var. Karşılıklı diyaloglar gerçekçi ve o kadar vurucu ki hafızaya kazınıyor. Çocukların olduğu her yerde kendiliğinden ortaya çıkan “saflık” ve savaşın olduğu her yerde yine kendiliğinden kaçınılmaz olarak meydana gelen “yıkım, huzursuzluk, kirlenme” sırt sırta ilerliyor öykülerde. Bu kontrast kurguyu dipdiri tutuyor. Öykülerin bel kemiğini güçlendiriyor. <br /><br />Ve en güzeli de, hemen hemen 9 öykünün hepsinde de unutulmayacak karakterler yaratmış Salinger. Ben artık aradan yıllar da geçse, yüzlerce kitap da okusam, binlerce kahramanla da tanışsam Esme, Seymour, Teddy, Eloise, De-Daumier Smith ve Ginnie’yi unutamam mesela, bundan eminim.<br /><br />İyi metin okumak isteyenlere kesinlikle tavsiye edeceğim bir kitap Salinger’dan Dokuz Öykü. <br /><br />10/10</span>

<div id="review_896765" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/896765" />
    <a title="Chloe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70078-chloe"><img alt="Chloe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580564408p2/70078.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/896765?book_show_action=true">Apr 26, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chloe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chloe" href="/user/show/70078-chloe">Chloe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70078-chloe?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70078-chloe?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70078-chloe?shelf=shorties">shorties</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70078-chloe?shelf=i_am_in_love">i_am_in_love</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70078-chloe?shelf=new-york">new-york</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer896765" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6670707324947529644">I was sitting at my cube farm today, moving numbers from one spreadsheet to another, cursing the internet tracking that keeps me from daytime Goodreading and daydreaming of pixies and unicorns when I received an email from my wife that utterly rocked my world. ":( Salinger's dead," read the short missive, and with that my world grew a little more gray. Normally news of celebrity death does little but placate my immense Schadenfreude, but Salinger's death is a serious blow to me and I feel compel</span>
  <span id="freeText6670707324947529644" style="display:none">I was sitting at my cube farm today, moving numbers from one spreadsheet to another, cursing the internet tracking that keeps me from daytime Goodreading and daydreaming of pixies and unicorns when I received an email from my wife that utterly rocked my world. ":( Salinger's dead," read the short missive, and with that my world grew a little more gray. Normally news of celebrity death does little but placate my immense Schadenfreude, but Salinger's death is a serious blow to me and I feel compelled to emote all over my computer screen (don't worry, I have tissues).<br /><br />Who remembers the moment when they first fell <i>passionately</i> in love with reading? I'm not talking about when you realized that reading was enjoyable, or a good distraction from your family, or a great way to spend a sunny day in the park. I'm talking about when you realized that this was it: life could throw anything at you and, as long as you had reading, you could cope and move on. That rather than simply entertaining, your world could be expanded and fleshed out by what you glean through a page- that this great human fuck-up can best be understood by placing yourself within the head of strangers and seeing the world through their eyes for a time.<br /><br />I can chart the exact instant this thought struck me- when I first finished reading Salinger's <i>Nine Stories</i>, particularly the utterly heart-breaking "A Perfect Day for Bananafish." To this day this book is still my favorite of his limited oeuvre and a surefire contender for Top 5 favorites of all time. While he is deservedly renowned for Holden Caulfield's teen angst, it is the subtle pathos of <i>Nine Stories</i> that marks him as an author without equal. <br /><br />The alienated Seymour Glass, who I always pictured as a stand-in for Salinger himself, and his tragic inability to connect with anyone but young children. The prescient Teddy, whose thinly-veiled Buddhism came years before the Beats began reading Suzuki. Esme, Charles and the damaged Sergeant X- all three of whom I feel an unceasing tenderness for. The idolized Chief and the heartbreak of Mary Hudson. All of these stories I can return to again and again, myself changed by the passing of time, and find something new and rewarding to take from them. Whether it is his absolutely perfect dialogue (I know of no other author who so accurately captures the rhythm and cadence of speech), his impulse (need?) to include a death in nearly all of his stories as if to remind us that even imaginary friends can get hit by buses, his endless attempts to put into words the passive disconnection from the rest of humankind that we all, at one point or other, feel overwhelmed by. There is more literary merit in this slim volume than the whole New York Times bestseller list.<br /><br />I've often harbored the dream of hanging out in Salinger's tiny New Hampshire village and somehow attracting the eye of the reclusive author- carrying groceries across the street or some such menial chore. We would get to talking and he would offer to read some of my meager works and, wonder of wonders, offer a few words of advice. You know, Daydreaming 101. Sadly this will never be. If there is a bright side to this tragic passing, it is that hopefully he’s been writing feverishly for the past 60 years and his estate will begin posthumously publishing. This is the only real kind of immortality available, and hopefully Salinger's words will be read for centuries to come.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6670707324947529644" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_9721481" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/9721481" />
    <a title="Mariel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/647547-mariel"><img alt="Mariel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451321467p2/647547.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/9721481?book_show_action=true">Nov 29, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mariel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mariel" href="/user/show/647547-mariel">Mariel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140237535" href="/book/show/5116.For_Esme_With_Love_and_Squalor_and_Other_Stories">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Rocky Dennis in Heaven</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Mariel by: </span>
      the girl from back then
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer9721481" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18367346579277807038">It has been a long time since I read Nine Stories. For once I don't care about getting older. This wasn't about that. So I started rereading Nine Stories in my car on my work lunch breaks. Getting through the day necessities stuff. I really needed an old friend. I was at a loss in a bad depths of despair kinda way that I cannot put in a meaningful way that will mean shit to anyone else. I remembered Nine Stories was good to me. I'm in no mood for anything more than that. Friends.<br/><br/>This probably wo</span>
  <span id="freeText18367346579277807038" style="display:none">It has been a long time since I read Nine Stories. For once I don't care about getting older. This wasn't about that. So I started rereading Nine Stories in my car on my work lunch breaks. Getting through the day necessities stuff. I really needed an old friend. I was at a loss in a bad depths of despair kinda way that I cannot put in a meaningful way that will mean shit to anyone else. I remembered Nine Stories was good to me. I'm in no mood for anything more than that. Friends.<br /><br />This probably won't make any sense to people who don't struggle with stupidity. There's a sick cloud feeling in my head that I get when I try to understand math, or something like that that requires logical thought. I never read instructions, either. I've been feeling this sick, cloudy feeling of anxious stupidity for no math related reasons. Just in general. It really sucks. <br /><br />So Salinger is my breath of fresh air from that feeling. I find I don't want to read anything but short stories right now.<br /><br />And other feelings... (I have a feeling I've mentioned this elsewhere on goodreads. Oh well.) When I was a kid (four?) I read this Peanuts comic strip. Charlie Brown was struggling to explain his mixed up feelings of being sad, happy, angry, all those free-range emotions, all at one time. I quoted that to my family to try to explain how I was feeling. They laughed their asses off. I slunk off to mood all by myself. It's really not about getting older. I'm still doing that. I've never found a better way than Charlie to explain that stuff. All of my moods are Peanuts broods... Anyway. Those feelings led to my reread. I needed them all. (You already wrote a review intro, Mariel. I can have more than one! Picking books is serious business! All at once!)<br /><br /> I remembered four of the nine stories well, the rest not at all. Shit. This throws new light on the ethics of my memory and reviewing books I read a long time ago. It's not a comfortable feeling. (Not that I won't still do it.) I'll include my old thoughts if I remember any.<br /><br /><b>A Perfect Day for Bananafish</b>:<br />Confession: I totally got into J.D. Salinger because of Robert Smith of The Cure. (According to youtube, there is a band named Bananafishbones.) <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=fa2Y1H9bIU8" rel="nofollow noopener">Bananafishbones - The Cure</a><br />I did remember this story. Of course I did. <br /><br />Old Mariel thoughts: This Muriel girl sucks. (I'm interested in all literary Mariels, Muriels, d other avariations on that theme. I draw the line at Mary. Mary-Ellens need not apply either. Who has the time?)<br /><br />I don't remember my old thoughts well enough. Did I relate to Seymour more? I think I relate to him more now just because I remember that old me had a massive inferiority complex when it came to precocious kids. Not that I enjoy the company of three year old girls (I've spent enough time in the company of ones related to me). Now? She wasn't all that precocious. Seymour is the permanently precocious. <br /><br />My ex once told me that kids stared at me because they sensed that I was one of them. I don't like him for saying that. <br /><br />I know too well the feeling of talking to people who respond as Seymour and Buddy do to their young girl friends. I get exactly those kinds of responses. It actually feels really lonely.<br /><br />This wasn't what was in my head while I was rereading in my car. What is special about the precocious? The ability to surprise. It isn't lack of artifice, or knowing rules. That simply isn't true. (The little girl reminded me of mind games that older girls would play on a boyfriend with her jealousy of the younger girl he was friends with.)<br /><br />It isn't good, the preoccupation with what other people notice. I don't want to think about relating to Seymour being offended when he thought the girl in the car was looking at his feet.<br /><br />I've said it all before about this thing about strangers. The great things about kids is that they are all strangers. There's something about talking to strangers who don't know all other threads to heap onto everything else to the point they can't listen to the <i>point</i> of what you're feeling. <br /><br />Maybe I don't really want to talk about suicide on goodreads. It has been in my life. There's a part of my brain that splits: 1. The person who dies. It's not up to me. Why would anyone want to go on living if they CAN'T go on living? 2. The people left behind. I've almost been them. I don't want to be them. That stuff I wrote earlier about threads... It's too much. This time... I was only feeling Seymour side. It's that kinda story.<br /><br />I didn't feel less lonely reading this one. I'm not sure what I felt except... I don't know. There's a time when leading someone else to look at bananafish isn't enough, when raising yourself isn't enough. Potential for what, anyway? <br /><br />Ruh roh, Raggy. If they are all this long this is gonna be a long ass review... <br /><br /><b>Uncle Wiggily in Connecticut</b><br />Confession: I can't spell Connecticut without cheating.<br />I didn't have invisible friends. I made friends out of inanimate objects. I've never really and truly stopped doing that. To myself I describe it as my Tom Hanks in Castaway with Wilson the Volley Ball moments. I'll stop ever doing it when I don't feel like that. Castawayed.<br /><br />I loved it when Jimmy gets killed and the little girl immediately replaces him with a new best friend. When a little kid I maimed my paper Care Bear doll (cut his leg off) and then couldn't stop crying over the loss of his leg (with vows of never hurting him again). I feel an affinity with Ramona. I'm sure she killed him for the tragedy of it all. (Or it really was the dog's fault.)<br /><br />I had a depressing feeling off this one. Maybe I get this from other Salinger females, like their good times end when they pretend not to be young anymore? I loved it when the mom, Eloise, wants reassurement of her past from her college friend, Mary Jane. What is the fun in being a dinner party person, anyway? Cocktails, dinner parties: same difference.<br /><br /><b>Just Before the War with the Eskimos</b>:<br />No confessions. (I'm not admitting to once writing a really bad story about an eskimo.)<br />I found reassuring so much the details like Selena coming back in a dress when she had been wearing shorts. (Even if it didn't annoy Ginnie as it normally would have.) I'd have been steamed. I hate waiting. I hate waiting soooo much. I hate it when people know you are waiting and run errands and shit. It gives me a sick and frustrated feeling. Anyway.<br /><br />I hope Ginnie will appreciate others noticing the interesting details, and how they put them, in more worthier people than Selena, her brother or his friend. What a shame. Younger me probably felt sad that she crushed on a guy who liked her (probably) prettier and more socially at ease sister. I probably also noted that Eric spoke like someone Holden would find annoying (his "grand" and all). I've always wanted to save people in stories from uninteresting characters. "You can do better!"<br /><br />P.s. The Eskimos will go after the French first. Maybe they don't wanna be named after a dessert cake any longer.<br /><br /><b>The Laughing Man</b>:<br />Confession: Talk of athetlics of any kind usually make my eyes glaze over. I admit to having an "Oh fuck" moment. I'm a jerk.<br /><br />I loved this story. I feel so much impatience with stories about beautiful people. It means fuck all to me to read the word beautiful. In this story the Chief and his Mary Hudson are beautiful like heroes of memory, and of stories. I feel embarrassed trying to describe this. I loved the Laughing Man stories that he told the kids. <br /><br />You know what? I haven't done a list in ages.<br /><br />Coolest bus drivers:<br />1. Chief<br />2. Bus-Driver Stu from The Adventures of Pete and Pete<br />3. Otto from The Simpsons<br /><br />It occurs to me now that Bus-Driver Stu holding the kids hostage to his relationship problems might've been a nod to this story. Awesome. Pete and Pete makes me happy like almost nothing else does. It's like ideals like how The Laughing Man does beauty. Not definitions, just living as day to day without reading the rules first. I suck at describing this. If I were them I'd never forget those stories either.<br /><br /><b>Down at the Dinghy</b>:<br />My confessions are embarrassing. I'm not gonna tell the story of when I tried to run away. It's not a nice story like this one.<br />I didn't remember this story despite once naming a bird I took care of after Boo Boo.<br />It must be great to have a mom like Boo Boo. <br /><br /><b>For Esme- with Love and Squalor</b>:<br />I liked this story much more as an adult than I did as a teenager. I was too close in age to Esme, perhaps. I was probably jealous of Esme because she was so smart for her age (I may as well confess that smart kids make me feel really bad about being a dumb adult). No poise either. But that doesn't matter at all. Old me! It was so sweet the way the brother and sister were with each other. How Esme missed her father (she was likely jealous of her mama so I wasn't that unusual as a teen, I guess)... Anyway, it made me happy how happy Buddy is to have his letter. I'm glad that he wrote his story and engaged more than his brother did with his girl friends. Friendships should be more than something the other cannot understand.<br /><br /><b>Pretty Mouth and Green My Eyes</b>:<br />I am losing steam with this one. I felt bad for Arthur... but I didn't want to spend time in their marriage problems. It makes me uncomfortable to hear about them. There's nothing I can do for them. You know?<br /><br />I did read that PJ Harvey made a nod to this story title in her song <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_f7CWCZIqvY" rel="nofollow noopener">Angelene</a>. This is one of my all-time favorite Peejay songs. Thanks, wikipedia trivia! <br /><br /><b>De Daumier-Smith's Blue Period</b>:<br />"The fact is always obvious much too late, but the most singular difference between happiness and joy is that happiness is a solid and joy a liquid."<br />I feel disconnected with much of this story, as with 'Pretty Mouth'. Blame my desperate impatientness for something... Maybe I want a life affirming day that beats any bananafish day, or anything. Something more sustaining. What the hell is gonna happen next? <br /><br /><b>Teddy</b>:<br />Teddy is right. It isn't so good that way, loving for the sake of loving. Sighs. But I do think too much. I wonder how Seymour would like talking to Teddy. He'd meditate and not give practiced answers, for sure. No pet nothing. Number two reasons this time. The little girl... Number one too. It's complicated.<br /><br />It did help. I'll always need more. I think, though, that Salinger didn't owe anybody to publish anything. (I'll always need more. That's exhausting.) Not any more than Seymour did with what he put out. I guess it's not always a perfect day for bananafish. I'm so glad these were published, though. I needed them like Buddy. It's not all a smile. I've already said that lame-o stuff about my Peanuts emotions. That's the best I've got. I wish I had better. Number one and number two reasons.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18367346579277807038" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Jeanette (Again)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/796425-jeanette-again"><img alt="Jeanette (Again)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1304653755p2/796425.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/78819123?book_show_action=true">Nov 23, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeanette (Again)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeanette (Again)" href="/user/show/796425-jeanette-again">Jeanette (Again)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/796425-jeanette-again?shelf=all-fiction">all-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/796425-jeanette-again?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/796425-jeanette-again?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/796425-jeanette-again?shelf=to-be-read-again">to-be-read-again</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/796425-jeanette-again?shelf=extraordinary-prose">extraordinary-prose</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer78819123" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7384926633728960148">This is one of "those" books. The ones where I turn the final page and sigh and wonder how I can convince other people that it's worth reading. Consider this: There are 30,520 ratings for this book here on Good Reads. The <i>average</i> of all those ratings is 4.18. Nothing I could say would be more convincing than that. Read it and marvel. <br/><br/>My two favorite stories are <i>For Esme--With Love and Squalor</i>, and <i>Down at the Dinghy</i>. I think I liked these best because I love the way Salinger writes about childre</span>
  <span id="freeText7384926633728960148" style="display:none">This is one of "those" books. The ones where I turn the final page and sigh and wonder how I can convince other people that it's worth reading. Consider this: There are 30,520 ratings for this book here on Good Reads. The <i>average</i> of all those ratings is 4.18. Nothing I could say would be more convincing than that. Read it and marvel. <br /><br />My two favorite stories are <i>For Esme--With Love and Squalor</i>, and <i>Down at the Dinghy</i>. I think I liked these best because I love the way Salinger writes about children. Tender and charming without ever being cutesy. I fell in love with the precocious Esme within the space of a few pages. I wanted a whole book about her! <br /><br /><i>Down at the Dinghy</i> features a sensitive little boy in self-exile on the family dinghy, and the way his mother gently coaxes him to come back up to the house. This is Salinger's true genius---creating perfect word pictures of ordinary events. <br /><br /><i>Nine Stories</i> should be required study for every creative writing program. An unpretentious, seemingly effortless, utterly original voice. If I could write just ONE story comparable to these, I'd be so puffed up with pride I'd bust right outta my corset!<br /><br /><br /><br />NB: The first story, <i>A Perfect Day for Bananafish</i>, can be quite jarring if you're not familiar with the Glass family featured in the novel <i>Franny and Zooey</i>. Seymour is the eldest child of that family, and always referred to in the past tense or at a remove in Salinger's other works. This is your one chance to see him while he's still alive. His sweet, gentle nuttiness with the little girl on the beach is especially touching.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7384926633728960148" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer133286487967418563">Reading this short story collection helps me to understand why Salinger was hounded to the ends of the earth in an effort to make him write again. His characters are so poignant and so real; his children so precocious and on the brink of something wholly indefinable. <br/><br/>I bought the book with a desire to revisit <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22836075.For_Esme___With_Love_And_Squalor' title='For Esme - With Love And Squalor by J.D. Salinger' rel='nofollow noopener'>For Esme - With Love And Squalor</a> and found it as captivating and moving as I had remembered, but the unexpected treasures of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15986803.The_Laughing_Man' title='The Laughing Man by J.D. Salinger' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Laughing Man</a> and Teddy left me breathless. Salinger knows co</span>
  <span id="freeText133286487967418563" style="display:none">Reading this short story collection helps me to understand why Salinger was hounded to the ends of the earth in an effort to make him write again. His characters are so poignant and so real; his children so precocious and on the brink of something wholly indefinable. <br /><br />I bought the book with a desire to revisit <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22836075.For_Esme___With_Love_And_Squalor" title="For Esme - With Love And Squalor by J.D. Salinger" rel="nofollow noopener">For Esme - With Love And Squalor</a> and found it as captivating and moving as I had remembered, but the unexpected treasures of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15986803.The_Laughing_Man" title="The Laughing Man by J.D. Salinger" rel="nofollow noopener">The Laughing Man</a> and Teddy left me breathless. Salinger knows complete sorry, desperation and irony when he finds it. As we peep into the world of his characters, who smoke their endless cigarettes, carry on their conversations of double meaning, and attempt to connect with others, we cannot help nodding in recognition of the knowledge that this is a microcosm of the human condition.</span>

<div id="review_2260611652" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2260611652" />
    <a title="Carmine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43396096-carmine"><img alt="Carmine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528454054p2/43396096.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2260611652?book_show_action=true">Apr 09, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carmine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carmine" href="/user/show/43396096-carmine">Carmine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788806197643" href="/book/show/9706002-nove-racconti">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43396096-carmine?shelf=raccolte-di-racconti">raccolte-di-racconti</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2260611652" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8059667557971729474"><b> Amaro sorriso sul viale del tramonto </b><br/><br/><i>"Ho con loro una affinità molto forte. Sono i miei genitori, voglio dire, e fra noi c'è un rapporto di dipendenza reciproca, di armonia reciproca e via dicendo.<br/>Voglio che si divertano mentre sono vivi perché a loro piace divertirsi...Ma loro non vogliono bene a me e a Hooper (è mia sorella) nello stesso modo. Voglio dire che non sono capaci di volerci bene così come siamo. Non sono capaci di volerci bene se non possono sempre cambiarci un poco. Amano le ragi</i></span>
  <span id="freeText8059667557971729474" style="display:none"><b> Amaro sorriso sul viale del tramonto </b><br /><br /><i>"Ho con loro una affinità molto forte. Sono i miei genitori, voglio dire, e fra noi c'è un rapporto di dipendenza reciproca, di armonia reciproca e via dicendo.<br />Voglio che si divertano mentre sono vivi perché a loro piace divertirsi...Ma loro non vogliono bene a me e a Hooper (è mia sorella) nello stesso modo. Voglio dire che non sono capaci di volerci bene così come siamo. Non sono capaci di volerci bene se non possono sempre cambiarci un poco. Amano le ragioni per le quali ci amano quasi quanto ci amano, e quasi sempre di più."</i><br /><br />Se il Salinger de "Il giovane Holden" tradisce un certo romanticismo incompreso - peculiarità del ragazzo con ancora la vita di fronte -, nei nove racconti qui presenti lo sguardo è quello amaro di chi ha perso tutto e il prezzo continua a esser pagato mentre si sorride al mondo.<br />La scrittura è talmente curata nel suo esser minimale da rasentare quasi l'ossessione; la capacità di evidenziare sottigliezze decisive è così chirurgica che par di vedere il bisturi in azione mentre incide la carne durante l'autopsia.<br /><br /><br />Un giorno ideale per i pescibanana ★★★★1/2<br />Il male ricevuto non si ripercuote su altri, ma su di sé: questa l’eredità degli anni ‘40.<br />Il racconto è un mosaico di piccoli dettagli da riscoprire in rilettura.<br /><br />Lo zio Wiggily nel Connecticut ★★★★★<br />Il dolore personale lo si riversa nell’altro perché il giovane non merita il privilegio dell’equilibrio e la meraviglia del divenire; non merita l’esercizio della fantasia.<br />Solo il gesto abietto dell’imposizione permette la comprensione del proprio dolore e, forse, il perdonare se stessi.<br /><br />Alla vigilia della guerra contro gli eschimesi ★★★1/2<br />Ancora la guerra sullo sfondo; ancora vite depauperate in un “sogno” che non si avvera.<br />O forse, come per la morte a causa del lanternino giapponese del racconto precedente, non esistono morti di serie A e di serie B. Né vite di serie A o di serie B.<br /><br />L’uomo ghignante ★★★★★<br />La necessità di avere eroi - invincibili, inarrestabili, inesorabili, freddi nella loro giustizia - è la prima maschera da gettare a terra nel momento in cui si passerà dall’altra parte.<br />Oggi è il giorno in cui inizieranno rinunce e compromessi; e i sogni saranno custodi di un’epoca prossima al congedo.<br /><br />Giù al dhingy ★★★<br />Racconto troppo dipendente da conoscenze storiche del periodo nonché componenti autobiografiche riguardanti l’autore (bello il tenero rapporto che si viene a creare sulla barca).<br /><br />Per Esmé: con amore e squallore ★★★★★<br />Sfumature sottili che affiorano e si estinguono nel giro di poche pagine; e la guerra, a far capolino dietro la vicenda, continua a emettere imperterrita il proprio verdetto.<br />Il rapporto triplice tra il sergente X, Esmé e Charles, che si dipana tra frasi tremendamente realistiche e gesti all’apparenza banali, è un piccolo capolavoro all’interno di un racconto che, come se non bastasse, approda a una parte finale anch’essa intrisa di umanità e grande compassione. <br />Un lettore si sente onorato a leggere cotanta bellezza.<br /><br />Bella bocca e occhi miei verdi ★★★★1/2<br />Amara l’incomunicabilità di matrimonio tra chi vive l’arte, e chi l’arte la subisce di riflesso.<br />Un mosaico di indizi a ricostruire una situazione sul filo del rasoio: come per il primo racconto, la capacità di gestione dei dettagli da parte di Salinger mette quasi paura.<br /><br />Il periodo blu di De Daumier-Smith ★★★<br />Simpatico racconto-aneddoto di taglio autobiografico che decreta la morte dell’individuo nel momento di massima contemplazione dell’arte.<br /><br />Teddy ★★★1/2<br />Storia un pochettino sbilanciata e dagli intenti non troppo chiari.<br />In mezzo alla confusione svetta il bellissimo monologo sull’amore “fasullo” genitoriale.<br /><br />P.S. Un grazie al caro Phil per aver portato avanti questa edificante esperienza di lettura condivisa.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8059667557971729474" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1618294100" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1618294100?book_show_action=true">Apr 23, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Helle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Helle" href="/user/show/6909470-helle">Helle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6909470-helle?shelf=american">american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6909470-helle?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6909470-helle?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1618294100" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer14060555133250825086">A slightly mixed bag of stories with some gems among them, notably <i>A Perfect Day for Bananafish</i> and, especially, <i>Teddy</i>. All the stories, even when I didn’t love them, had small moments of either brilliance or craziness. And Salinger was a master of the quirky, zany dialogue full of 50s slang, non sequiturs and sarcastic repartees. There were laugh-out-loud moments, there were bizarre moments where I had no idea what he was on about, there was hyperbole galore, and there was much of the 1940s swa</span>
  <span id="freeText14060555133250825086" style="display:none">A slightly mixed bag of stories with some gems among them, notably <i>A Perfect Day for Bananafish</i> and, especially, <i>Teddy</i>. All the stories, even when I didn’t love them, had small moments of either brilliance or craziness. And Salinger was a master of the quirky, zany dialogue full of 50s slang, non sequiturs and sarcastic repartees. There were laugh-out-loud moments, there were bizarre moments where I had no idea what he was on about, there was hyperbole galore, and there was much of the 1940s swagger that I first met (and loved) in <i>The Catcher in the Rye</i>. Once or twice I suspected the narrator was more or less unreliable, and this, too, of course (viz. to some extent Holden) Salinger masters. <br /><br />A few choice excerpts:<br /><br />From ‘A Perfect Day for Bananafish’:<br /><br /><i>She was a girl who for a ringing phone dropped exactly nothing. She looked as if her phone had been ringing continually ever since she had reached puberty</i>.<br /><br />From ‘Just before the War with the Eskimos’:<br /><br /><i>He was staring down, with his slack mouth ajar, at his injured finger. ‘What?’ he said.<br /><br />‘How did you cut it?’<br /><br />‘Goddam if <b>I</b> know’, he said, his inflection implying that the answer to that question was hopelessly obscure. ‘I was lookin’ for something in the goddam wastebasket and it was fulla razor blades.’<br /><br />‘You Selena’s brother?’ Ginnie asked.<br /><br />‘Yeah, Christ, I’m bleedin’ to death. Stick around. I may need a goddam transfusion.’</i><br /><br />From ‘Teddy’:<br /><br /><i>Mr. McArdle played leading roles on no fewer than three daytime radio serials when he was in New York, and he had what might be called a third-class leading man’s speaking voice: narcissistically deep and resonant, functionally prepared at a moment’s notice to out-male anyone in the same room with it, if necessary even a small boy.</i><br /><br />A comment by precocious Teddy himself (about his parents):<br /><br /><i>I mean they don’t seem able to love us just the way we are. They don’t seem able to love us unless they can keep changing us a little bit. They love their reasons for loving us almost as much as they love us, and most of the time more.</i><br /><br />And then of course, there’s the fantastically titled ‘For Esmé – with Love and Squalor’, which has a number of marvelous lexical misunderstandings.<br /><br />Recommended for people who like Salinger and short stories.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14060555133250825086" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
